فصل في احكام الوضوء الخفيفة. ولا يجب ايصال الماء الى المسترسل من الشعر عن دائرة الوجه (۷۸) ولا الى ما انكتم قدمه فأبصر النبى الله الله فقال : ارجع فأحسن وضوئک ، فرجع ثم صلی ۔ (مسلم شریف ، باب وجوب استیعاب جميع اجزا محل الطهارة ،ص ۱۲۱، نمبر ۵۷۶/۲۴۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ تھوڑی سی کھال بھی سوکھی نہیں رہنی چاہئے ۔ (۲) اس حدیث کے اشارے سے پتہ چلتا ہے کہ گھنی ڈاڑھی کے اوپر پانی پہنچ جانا کافی ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال دخل علی علی یعنی ابی طالب و قد اهرق الماء ..... ثم أخذ بكفه اليمنى قبضة من ماء فصبها على ناصيته فتركها تستن على وجهه ۔ (ابوداود شریف ، باب صفة وضوء النبی ، ص ۲۷، نمبر ۱۱۷) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ پیشانی کے شروع سے تھوڑی کے نیچے تک پانی بہایا ، جس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ ڈاڑھی کے اوپر پانی بہنا کافی ہے۔ (۳) اس قول تابعی میں اس کی صراحت ہے کہ گھنی ڈاڑھی پر پانی بہہ جانا کافی ہے۔ عن ابی عالیة قال حسبک ما سأل من وجهک علی لحیتک ۔ (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة ، باب من كان لا تخلل لحية و یقول یکفیک ما سال علیها ، ج اول ص ۲۱، نمبر ۱۲۰) اس قول تابعی میں ہے کہ ڈاڑھی پر جو پانی گرے وہی کافی ہے۔ (۴) اور لٹکی ہوئی ڈاڑھی میں خلال کرنا مستحب ہے، اس کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے ۔عن انس بن مالک ان رسول الله الا الله كان اذا توضأ أخذ كفا من ماء فأدخله تحت حنكه فخلل به لحيته ، و قال هكذا امرني ربی عز و جل ۔ (ابوداود شریف ، باب تخلیل اللحیة، ص ۱۳۱، نمبر ۱۴۵ ترمذی شریف، باب تخلیل اللحیة، ص ۱۴ نمبر ۳۱ رابن ماجه شریف، باب ماجاء فی تقلیل اللحية، ص ۶۳ نمبر ۴۲۹) (۵) اس حدیث میں بھی ہے کہ لٹکی ہوئی ڈاڑھی میں خلال فرمایا کرتے تھے عن ابن عمر قال كان رسول الله لا اذا توضأعرک عارضیه بعض العرك ثم شبك لحيته بأصابعه من تحتها - (ابن ماجہ شریف ، باب ما جاء تخلیل اللحیتہ ص ۶۳ نمبر ۴۲۳۲) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ ڈاڑھی کے نیچے سے خلال کرتے تھے ۔ (4) اس عمل صحابی سے استحباب کا پتہ چلتا ہے۔ ان ابن عمر كان اذا توضأ يعرک عارضیه و يشبك لحيته باصابعه احيانا و یترک احیانا (سنن بیہقی ، باب عرک العارضین ، ج اول ،ص ۹۲ نمبر (۲۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کبھی ڈاڑھی میں خلال کرتے تھے اور کبھی چھوڑ دیتے تھے، جس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ گھنی ڈاڑھی ہو تو اندر پانی پہچانا فرض نہیں ہے۔ (۷) عبد الرحمن بن ابى ليلى قال : ان استطعت ان تبلغ بالماء اصول اللحية فافعل . (مصنف ابن ابی هیة ، افی غسل اللحية في الوضوء، ج اول ص ۲۲ نمبر ۱۲۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ ڈاڑھی ہلکی ہو اور بال کی جڑ تک پانی پہونچا سکتے ہو تو وہاں تک پانی پہونچاو۔ لغت : الکثیہ گھنی ڈاڑھی ۔ الخفيفة : هلكی ۔المستر سل : رسل سے مشتق ہے لٹکا ہوا ہونا۔ دائرۃ الوجہ : چہرے کا دائرہ ،تھوڑی تک کے حصے کو چہرے کا دائرہ کہتے ہیں۔
Section on the rulings of wudu. The light (beard). And it is not obligatory to deliver water to the hanging hair from the circle of the face (78). Nor to what is hidden? ... Then the Prophet saw him and said: 'Go back and perform wudu properly', so he went back and then prayed. (Muslim) In this hadith, a small bit of skin must not remain dry. (2) From the indication of this hadith, it is clear that for a thick beard, water reaching over it is sufficient. From Ibn Abbas: Ali entered... then he took in his right palm a handful of water and poured it on his forelock and let it flow over his face. (Abu Dawud) This hadith shows that water flowing from the beginning of the forehead to below the chin is sufficient, so water flowing over the beard is sufficient. (3) This saying of a Tabi'i clarifies that water flowing over a thick beard is sufficient. From Abi 'Aliya: 'It suffices you what flows from your face onto your beard.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba) This saying of a Tabi'i says that water that falls on the beard is sufficient. (4) And for a hanging beard, takhleel (passing fingers through) is recommended (mustahabb). For this, there is this hadith from Anas ibn Malik: The Prophet, when he performed wudu, took a handful of water, put it under his chin, and passed it through his beard, and said: 'Thus my Lord commanded me.' (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah) (5) In this hadith also, he used to do takhleel for the hanging beard. From Ibn Umar: The Prophet, when he performed wudu, rubbed his cheeks a little, then interlaced his beard with his fingers from beneath. (Ibn Majah) This hadith shows that he did takhleel from under the beard. (4) From this action of a Companion, recommendation is understood. That Ibn Umar, when he performed wudu, would rub his cheeks and interlace his beard with his fingers sometimes and leave it sometimes. (Bayhaqi) This hadith shows that sometimes he would do takhleel and sometimes leave it, which means that if the beard is thick, delivering water inside is not obligatory. (7) Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla said: 'If you can reach the roots of the beard with water, do so.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba) This effect says that if the beard is light and you can reach the roots of the hair with water, then deliver water there. Vocabulary: al-kathifa: thick beard. al-khafifa: light. al-mustarsil: derived from 'rasl', meaning hanging. Da'irat al-wajh: the circle of the face, i.e., the part up to the chin is called the circle of the face.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs on washing the beard during wudu. The author begins by stating the general ruling: it is not obligatory to deliver water to the hanging hair that extends below the circle of the face (i.e., the part of the beard that hangs below the chin). The circle of the face is defined as the area from the forehead to the chin. The text then cites several hadith and athar to support the distinction between a thick beard and a hanging beard. For a thick beard, merely causing water to flow over the external hairs is sufficient; there is no obligation to reach the skin or roots. This is derived from the hadith where the Prophet commanded a man who left a dry spot the size of a nail to redo his wudu, implying that any dryness invalidates wudu, but for a thick beard, the water flowing over is considered sufficient for the entire beard. The practice of Ali and the statement of Abi 'Aliya reinforce this. For a hanging beard (i.e., one that extends below the chin), it is recommended (mustahabb) to pass fingers through it (takhleel) to ensure water reaches the skin, based on the hadith of Anas where the Prophet specifically did takhleel and stated that his Lord commanded him. However, the hadith of Ibn Umar shows that he sometimes did takhleel and sometimes left it, indicating that it is not obligatory but recommended. Finally, for a light or thin beard, one should attempt to reach the roots if possible. The passage thus clarifies the Hanafi position: the fard is to wash the skin of the face; for a thick beard, the surface suffices; for a hanging beard, takhleel is sunnah; for a thin beard, one should aim to wet the roots. This is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
المسترسل من الشعر (al-mustarsil min al-sha'r): The hair that hangs down from the circle of the face (below the chin).
اللحية (al-lihyah): The beard; includes hair on the cheeks and chin.
التخليل (al-takhleel): Passing fingers through the beard to ensure water reaches the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on performing takhleel (running fingers through) the beard during wudu?
A) It is obligatory for all beards.
B) It is recommended (mustahabb) for a hanging beard, but not obligatory.
C) It is prohibited.
D) It is only for thick beards.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on the beard in wudu distinguishes between the part within the face circle (obligatory to wet) and hanging part (takhleel recommended), based on hadith and practice of the Companions.

Ruling on Washing the Inner Part of the Lips in Wudu
Explains that the hidden part of the lips when closed is not obligatory to wash

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing the inner lips in wudu, distinguish between fard and sunnah parts, and cite the Quranic and hadith proofs.

Core Idea
Only the visible part of the lips is fard to wash; the inner hidden part is sunnah.

The Prophet ﷺ said, 'Ten are from the fitrah,' including rinsing the mouth. This lesson examines how the Hanafi school uses the Quranic command to 'wash your faces' to determine that the hidden part of the lips is not obligatory. We will explore the proofs and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing the lips during wudu. Determine the hukm (ruling) for the inner part when the mouth is closed: is it fard, wajib, sunnah, etc.?
The passage clearly states it is not wajib; it is sunnah.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the Quranic verse cited: 'Wash your faces' (Al-Ma'idah 5:6). Note how the Hanafis interpret the word 'wujuh' (faces).
The verse is mutawatir (qat'i thubut) but its interpretation is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the word 'faces' implies only the visible part. The inner lips are not visible when the mouth is closed, so they are excluded from the obligation.
This is a linguistic argument based on the root 'wajh' meaning 'to face or be apparent'.
4
Identify the secondary dalil
The passage also cites the hadith on fitrah that includes madmadah (rinsing the mouth) as a sunnah, not a fard, supporting that washing inside the mouth is recommended.
This hadith is ahad but used as supporting evidence.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and thamarah
The Shafi'i school considers the entire lip as part of the face. The practical difference: Hanafis do not require washing the inner lips; Shafi'is do. The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
The difference stems from the interpretation of 'wujuh'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۷۸) اور منہ بند کرتے وقت اندر چھپے ہوئے ہونٹوں کا دھونا بھی واجب نہیں ہے۔ من الشفتين عند الانضمام (۷۹) ولو انضمت الاصابع او طال الظفر فغطّى الانملة او كان فيه ما تشریح : دونوں ہونٹوں کو بند کرتے وقت جو حصہ چھپ جاتا ہے اس کو وضو میں دھونا واجب نہیں ، بلکہ سنت ہے، اور جو حصہ باہر رہ جاتا ہے اس کو دھونا فرض ہے۔ وجه : (۱) وضو میں منہ کے اندر دھونا فرض نہیں ہے صرف باہر دھونا فرض ہے ، اور ہونٹ بند کرنے کے بعد جو حصہ اندر رہ جاتا ہے وہ منہ کے اندر کا حصہ ہے اس لئے اس کو دھونا فرض نہیں ہے، البتہ سنت ہے۔ (۲) اس حدیث میں ہے۔ عن عائشة قالت قال رسول الله له عشر من الفطرة ، قص الشارب ، و اعفاء اللحية ، و السواك، واستنشاق الماء ، و قص الاظفار، وغسل البراجم ، و نتف الابط ، و حلق العانة ، و انتقاض الماء.. قال زكريا قال مصعب : و نسیت العاشرة الا ان تكون المضمضة - (مسلم شریف، باب خصال الفطرة ، ص ۱۲۵، نمبر ۶۰۴/۲۶ ابوداود شریف ، باب السواک من الفطرة ، ص ۱۹، نمبر ۵۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ مضمضہ کرنا یعنی منہ میں پانی ڈالنا سنت ہے۔(۲) اس آیت کے اشارے میں بھی ہے کہ وضو میں وجوہ کو دھوؤ اور وجوہ موجہت سے مشتق ہے، جس کا مطلب ہے کہ جو سامنے نظر آئے اس کو دھوؤ ، اور ہونٹ کے اندر کا حصہ سامنے نظر نہیں آتا اس لئے اس کو دھونا واجب نہیں ہوگا۔ آیت یہ ہے۔ یا ایها الذين آمنو اذا قمتم الى الصلوة فاغسلوا وجوهكم و ايديكم الى المرافق۔( آیت ۶ ،سورۃ المائدۃ ۵) لغت انتم : چھپانا، المتم: چھپ جاتا ہو شتین : دونوں ہونٹ۔ انضمام: ضم سے مشتق ہے ملتے وقت۔
Translation: (78) And when the mouth is closed, washing the inner hidden part of the lips is not obligatory. From the two lips when they come together (79) (and if the fingers are joined or the nail becomes long and covers the fingertip or there is something on it...) Explanation: When the two lips are closed, the part that becomes hidden is not obligatory to wash in wudu, rather it is sunnah. And the part that remains outside is fard to wash. Reasoning: (1) In wudu, washing the inside of the mouth is not fard, only the outside is fard. After closing the lips, the part that remains inside is part of the inside of the mouth, so washing it is not fard, but it is sunnah. (2) In this hadith: from Aisha that the Prophet said: 'Ten are from the fitrah: trimming the mustache, letting the beard grow, using the siwak, sniffling water (into the nose), clipping the nails, washing the finger joints, plucking the armpit hair, shaving the pubic hair, and washing with water...' Zakariyya said: 'Mus'ab said: I forgot the tenth unless it is rinsing the mouth (madmadah).' (Muslim, Abu Dawud) This hadith indicates that rinsing the mouth (madmadah) is sunnah. (2) In the indication of this verse also, in wudu wash your faces ('wujuh'), and 'wujuh' is derived from 'wajh' meaning what is visible. So wash what is seen, and the inner part of the lips is not seen, so washing it is not obligatory. The verse is: 'O you who believe, when you stand for prayer, wash your faces and your hands up to the elbows.' (Al-Ma'idah 5:6) Vocabulary: 'anatm' (?) : to hide, 'al-mutamm' : becomes hidden, 'al-shatayn' : the two lips, 'indimam' : derived from 'damm', meaning when they come together.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on washing the lips during wudu. The author first gives the translation of point (78), stating that washing the inner hidden part of the lips when the mouth is closed is not obligatory. Then the Arabic text of point (79) is introduced, which also addresses the lips and then moves to fingers, but the commentary focuses on the lips. The explanation emphasizes that the fard is to wash the visible part of the face, as implied by the word 'wujuh' in the Quranic verse (5:6), which is derived from 'wajh' meaning 'face' or 'that which is apparent'. Therefore, the inner, hidden part of the lips is not part of the face that must be washed; it is merely sunnah to wash it, as indicated by the hadith on the fitrah which includes madmadah (rinsing the mouth) as a sunnah act. The Hanafi position is that washing the inside of the mouth is not part of the fard of wudu; only the outer lips are fard. The inner part is sunnah (mustahabb). This is based on the literal interpretation of 'wujuh' and the classification of madmadah as sunnah.

Key Terms
وجوه (wujuh): The face, specifically the visible outer parts when the eyes are open and mouth is closed.
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Rinsing the mouth with water, considered a sunnah act in wudu by Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling on washing the inner part of the lips (the part that is hidden when the mouth is closed) during wudu?
A) It is fard (obligatory).
B) It is sunnah (recommended).
C) It is makruh.
D) It is haram.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi wudu, only the visible outer lips are fard to wash; the hidden inner part is sunnah, based on the literal meaning of 'faces' in the Quran.

Ensuring Water Reaches the Skin: Barriers in Wudu
Explains the obligation to wash under nails and between fingers, and remove barriers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that delivering water to every part of the skin of wudu limbs is fard, and identify when barriers like long nails, joined fingers, or dough require removal or special action.

Core Idea
Every part of the skin on wudu limbs must be wetted; even a dry spot the size of a nail invalidates wudu.

This passage tackles a practical concern in wudu: physical barriers that prevent water from reaching the skin. The author explains that delivering water to the skin is fard, so joined fingers, long nails, and substances like dough must be addressed. A hadith from Umar reinforces that no area, however small, may remain dry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is wudu. The ruling is that delivering water to the skin of the washed limbs is fard. This is the core principle underlying the specific cases.
Barriers block water, so they must be removed or water must be forced to the skin.
2
List the barriers mentioned
The passage names three: (1) fingers pressed together so water cannot seep between, (2) long nails covering the skin underneath, (3) substances like dough or paint stuck on the skin.
Each case requires either removing the barrier or ensuring water reaches the skin.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Umar: a man left a dry spot the size of a nail on his foot; the Prophet ordered him to repeat wudu. This is found in Sahih Muslim.
The hadith is an ahad hadith, so its thubut is zanni, but its meaning is clear.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to redo wudu for a tiny dry area proves that any dry spot invalidates wudu. Therefore, water must reach every part of the skin, and barriers that prevent this must be addressed.
The implication is that obligation is absolute, not excused by size or commonness of the barrier.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds this as mufta bihi from Zahir al-Riwayah. There is no intra-madhhab dispute on the principle; it is agreed upon across schools as well (muttafaq alayh).
The ruling applies equally to all types of barriers, tangible or due to limb positioning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۷۹) اور اگر انگلیاں ملی ہوئی ہوں یا ناخن نے دراز ہو کر پوروں کو ڈھانپ لیا ہو یا ناخن میں ایسی چیز ہو جو پانی کو کھال تک پہنچنے میں منع کرے مثلا گوندھا ہوا آٹا تو اس کے نیچے پانی پہونچا نا واجب ہے۔ تشریح : یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ وضو میں کھال تک پانی پہنچانا فرض ہے، اس لئے انگلیاں اس طرح ملی ہوئیں ہیں کہ آسانی سے کھال تک پانی نہیں پہنچتا تو وہاں تک پانی پہنچانا ضروری ہوگا۔ یا ناخن لمبا ہو گیا جس کی وجہ سے ناخن کے اندر کھال تک پانی نہیں پہنچتا ہو تو کھال تک پانی پہنچانا ضروری ہوگا۔ یا گوندھا ہوا آٹا لگا ہوا ہے یا پینٹ لگا ہوا ہے جسکی وجہ سے کھال تک پانی نہیں پہنچ پاتا ہے تو اس کو زائل کر کے کھال تک پانی پہنچانا واجب ہوگا ورنہ بال برابر بھی سوکھی رہ گئی تو وضو نہیں ہوگا اور پھر نماز بھی نہیں ہوگی۔ وجه : (1) اخبرني عمر بن الخطاب ان رجلا توضأ فترك موضع ظفر على قدمه فأبصر النبي فقال : ارجع فأحسن وضوئک ، فرجع ثم صلی ۔ (مسلم شریف ، باب وجوب استيعاب جميع اجزاء محل الطهارة ،ص ۱۲۱، نمبر ۵۷۶/۲۴۳ ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ تھوڑی سی کھال بھی سوکھی نہیں رہنی چاہئے ۔(۲) ۔ عن عبد الله بن عمرو قال صلى الله رجعنا مع رسول الله عليه من مكة الى المدينة حتى اذا كنا بماء بالطريق تعجل قوم عند العصر فتوضأ وا و
Translation: (79) And if the fingers are joined (pressed together) or the nail became long and covered the fingertip, or there is something on it that prevents water from reaching the skin, like dough, then it is obligatory to deliver water under it. Explanation: This issue is based on the principle that in wudu, delivering water to the skin is fard. Therefore, if the fingers are joined in such a way that water cannot easily reach the skin, then it is necessary to deliver water there. Or if the nail became long such that water does not reach the skin under the nail, then it is necessary to deliver water to the skin. Or if dough or paint is attached that prevents water from reaching the skin, then it is obligatory to remove it and deliver water to the skin; otherwise, if even the size of a hair remains dry, the wudu will not be valid, and then the prayer will not be valid. Proof: (1) Umar ibn al-Khattab informed me that a man performed wudu and left a spot the size of a nail on his foot. The Prophet saw him and said: 'Go back and perform wudu properly', so he went back and then prayed. (Muslim) This hadith indicates that even a small amount of skin must not remain dry. (2) From Abdullah ibn Amr: We returned with the Messenger of Allah from Mecca to Madinah until we came to a water source on the road; some people hurried for Asr prayer and performed wudu...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on ensuring water reaches the skin in wudu when there are physical barriers. The author presents the principle: delivering water to the skin is fard. Therefore, if fingers are pressed together preventing water from seeping between them, it is obligatory to separate them or ensure water reaches the skin. Similarly, if a nail is long so that the skin beneath is not wetted, water must be forced under it. If there is a substance like dough or paint that blocks water, it must be removed. The hadith of Umar about a man who left a dry spot the size of a nail emphasizes that no part of the skin, however small, should remain dry. This hadith is used as evidence that even a tiny dry area invalidates wudu. The second hadith is cut off but likely reinforces the point. The Hanafi position is clear: the obligation is to wash every part of the limbs specified in wudu; any barrier, whether natural (like joined fingers or long nails) or accidental (like dough), must be addressed to ensure complete wetness. This is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الظفر (al-zufr): The nail; if long, it prevents water from reaching the skin underneath, so water must be forced under.
العجين (al-'ajin): Dough; an example of a substance that blocks water, requiring removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person has dough (uncooked dough) stuck under their nails during wudu?
A) Wudu is valid regardless because the dough is not a barrier.
B) The dough must be removed and water must reach the skin; otherwise wudu is invalid.
C) It is permissible to leave it, and wudu is valid.
D) Wudu is invalid only if the dough is more than a dirham in size.

Key Takeaway
Delivering water to every part of the skin of wudu limbs is fard; any barrier, natural or accidental, that prevents this must be addressed for wudu to be valid.

Washing the Inner Lips: Fard or Sunnah?
Ruling on washing the inner part of the lips when closed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing the inner part of the lips during wudu and explain the evidence from hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
Only the visible part of the lips is fard; the hidden part is sunnah.

In wudu, the obligation is to wash the face. But what about the inside of the lips? This lesson from Thamaratun Najah clarifies that when the lips are closed, only the outer part is obligatory (fard); the inner part is recommended (sunnah), based on the hadith of fitrah and the linguistic interpretation of 'face' in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine exactly what part of the lips is being discussed: the inner part when the lips are closed. State the Hanafi ruling: it is sunnah (recommended) to wash, not fard (obligatory). The outer part is fard.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the two proofs cited: the hadith of fitrah (Muslim) listing madmada (rinsing mouth) among the sunan, and the Quranic verse (5:6) 'wash your faces' – the word 'wujuh' indicates what is visible.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For the hadith: the listing of madmada among the sunan of fitrah shows it is sunnah, not fard. For the verse: 'wujuh' (faces) is derived from 'wajh' meaning 'what is visible', so the inner part (hidden when lips closed) is not included in the fard.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school considers rinsing the mouth fard, but the Hanafi school distinguishes using the linguistic interpretation and the weaker authority of the hadith (ahad).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) based on the hadith and Quranic interpretation. The ruling is clear: inner part sunnah, outer part fard.

Section on rulings of wudu. From the two lips when they close (79). Explanation: When closing the two lips, the part that is hidden is not obligatory to wash in wudu, rather it is sunnah, and the part that remains outside is fard to wash. Reasoning: (1) In wudu, washing the inside of the mouth is not fard, only the outside is fard. When the lips are closed, the part that remains inside is part of the inside of the mouth, so washing it is not fard, but it is sunnah. (2) This hadith states: From Aisha, she said the Messenger of Allah said: Ten are from fitrah: trimming the mustache, letting the beard grow, using miswak, rinsing the nose, clipping the nails, washing the finger joints, plucking the armpit hair, shaving the pubic hair, and washing with water. Zakaria said: Mus'ab said: I forgot the tenth except that it is rinsing the mouth. (Muslim, Kitab al-Taharah, no. 604/26; Abu Dawud, no. 53). This hadith indicates that rinsing the mouth (madhada) is sunnah. (3) The indication of this verse: 'O you who believe, when you rise for prayer, wash your faces and your hands to the elbows.' (Al-Ma'idah 5:6). The word 'wujuh' is derived from 'wajh' meaning what is visible. So the inner part of the lips is not visible, thus washing it is not obligatory. Lexicon: 'an' means to hide, 'iltama' means becomes hidden, 'shifshif' means two lips, 'indimam' means closing.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This is a ruling_with_proof passage from Thamaratun Najah discussing the wudu ruling regarding the lips. The author states that when the lips are closed, the hidden part is not fard but sunnah to wash, while the visible part is fard. The proof is twofold: first, the inside of the mouth is not fard, so the concealed lip area is analogous; second, the hadith of fitrah lists madmada (rinsing mouth) as sunnah, and the verse 'wash your faces' implies only what is visible. The lexical notes explain key terms: 'an' (to hide), 'iltama' (becomes hidden), 'shifshif' (two lips), 'indimam' (closing).

Key Terms
الشفتين (ash-shafatayn): The two lips; specifically the outer and inner parts considered in wudu.
الانضمام (al-indimam): The closing or bringing together of the two lips.
المضمضة (al-madmada): Rinsing the mouth with water, considered a sunnah act in wudu.

Key Takeaway
The inner part of the lips when closed is sunnah to wash in wudu, based on the hadith of fitrah and the linguistic interpretation of 'face'.

Washing Under Fingers and Nails in Wudu
Ruling on washing interlocked fingers and under long nails or obstructions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation to wash skin under interlocked fingers, long nails, and obstructions, and apply the hadith evidence to similar cases.

Core Idea
Every part of the skin must be wet; interlocked fingers and long nails require extra care.

Wudu requires water to reach every part of the specified limbs. This passage specifies three common situations where water might not reach: fingers tightly pressed together, nails covering the fingertip, and obstructions like dough or paint. The ruling is clear: any dry spot, however small, invalidates wudu. Two hadith provide the evidence, emphasizing the need for thoroughness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
Locate the three scenarios: interlocked fingers, long nails covering the fingertip, and substances blocking water (dough, paint).
These are all situations where water may fail to reach the skin naturally.
2
Derive the legal principle
From the scenarios, extract the underlying rule: water must reach all skin of the wudu limbs. Any obstruction must be removed.
This principle is stated explicitly in the commentary.
3
Analyze the first hadith
Read the hadith about the man leaving a nail-sized spot on his foot. Identify the command: 'return and perfect your wudu.'
This shows even a small dry spot requires repetition.
4
Analyze the second hadith
Read the hadith about dry heels. Identify the warning: 'Woe to the heels from the Fire. Perfect the wudu.'
Emphasizes the severity of leaving even the heels dry.
5
Conclude the ruling
Combine the principle and evidence: it is obligatory (fard) to wash all skin, including under nails and between fingers. If any spot remains dry, wudu is invalid.
The passage states 'a small part of skin remaining dry invalidates wudu.'

And if the fingers are interlocked or the nail became long covering the fingertip, or there is something on it that prevents water like dough, it is obligatory to wash what is underneath. Explanation: This matter is based on the principle that in wudu, reaching water to the skin is fard. So if fingers are closed together such that water does not easily reach the skin, then reaching water there is necessary. Or if the nail became long so that water does not reach the skin under the nail, then reaching water is necessary. We came to them and their heels were dry, water had not touched them. The Messenger of Allah said: Woe to the heels from the Fire. Perfect the wudu. (Muslim, no. 570/241). This hadith indicates that a small part of skin must not remain dry, otherwise wudu is invalid. Principle: A small part of skin remaining dry in the limbs of wudu invalidates wudu. Dirt and Insect Droppings Do Not Prevent Water
Ruling that minor dirt and insect excrement do not block water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that minor dirt and insect droppings do not prevent water from reaching the skin during wudu, and identify when a substance actually blocks water.

Core Idea
Minor dirt and insect droppings do not invalidate wudu — only substances that physically block water matter.

You may wonder whether a small amount of dirt under the nail or a flea dropping on your skin breaks your wudu. This lesson clarifies that such negligible substances do not prevent water from reaching the skin, so wudu remains valid. The Hanafi school focuses on the effect of the substance: does it actually block water? If not, it is excused.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is performing wudu. The ruling is that wudu is valid despite the presence of dirt or insect droppings, as stated in the text: 'dirt and the droppings of fleas and the like do not prevent water.'
2
Assess the Substance's Quantity and Effect
Only substances that form a physical barrier preventing water from contacting the skin invalidate wudu. The passage explicitly says these are in such small quantity that they do not prevent water. Judge whether the substance is minuscule and does not repel water.
A thick layer of paint or wax would block water, but a tiny speck does not.
3
Apply to Examples
Dirt under the nail? Excused. Flea droppings? Excused. However, a sticky resin or dried glue must be removed because it forms a barrier.
The key is whether water can reach the skin directly under the substance.

Moving a Tight Ring During Wudu
Ruling that a tight ring must be moved to allow water underneath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on moving a tight ring during wudu, cite the evidence from hadith, and explain why it is wajib.

Core Idea
A tight ring must be moved during wudu to ensure water reaches the skin below.

This lesson addresses a common practical issue: what if you are wearing a tight ring during wudu? The Hanafi school rules that it is obligatory to move it so that water reaches the skin, based on prophetic practice. We will examine the ruling and its evidence from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act of moving a tight ring during wudu. What is the ruling? It is wajib (obligatory).
The passage states 'wajib tahrik al-khatam ad-dayyiq'.
2
Locate the hadith evidence
Two hadith are cited. The first commands takhlil between fingers; the second reports that the Prophet moved his ring. Identify these as the proof.
Hadith from Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the obligation? The command for takhlil implies that water must reach all skin, and moving the ring is an extension of this principle. The Prophet's action confirms it.
The ruling is based on the general principle of ensuring water reaches every part.
4
Apply the ruling in practice
If the ring is tight, you must rotate it so water passes underneath. If it is loose, moving is not required.
The ruling also extends to earrings and other tight ornaments.

Washing Cracks in Feet When Harmful
Ruling on passing water over cracked feet if washing causes harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing cracked feet when harmful and apply the analogy to bandaged wounds.

Core Idea
When washing a wound or crack causes harm, wiping over the bandage suffices — necessity overrides the requirement of direct water contact.

In wudu, normally every part of the foot must be washed with water reaching the skin. However, when a person has cracks or wounds that make washing harmful, Islamic law provides a concession. This lesson explains the Hanafi position that passing water over the bandage or medicine is sufficient, based on proof from hadith and analogy to bandaged wounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act in question is washing cracked feet during wudu. The ruling is that if washing causes harm, it is permissible (jā'iz) to merely pass water over the bandage or medicine without the water reaching the skin.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two proofs: (1) the hadith about heels from Sahih Muslim emphasizing the obligation of washing feet but implying that when water cannot reach due to a cover, wiping suffices; and (2) the hadith of Jabir about bandaging a wound and wiping over it.
The hadith texts are partially garbled in the source.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
From the hadith of Jabir, the Prophet (ﷺ) indicated that wiping over the bandage is sufficient. The Hanafi school extends this ruling by qiyas (analogy) to cracked feet with medicine, since both involve a constraint (harm) that prevents direct washing.
The operative illa (effective cause) is the presence of harm or difficulty in washing the affected area.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, there is no significant disagreement. The ruling is considered muttafaq alayh among the Hanafi imams. Other schools may have different positions, but this lesson focuses on the Hanafi view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that passing water over the bandage or medicine suffices. This is based on the principle that necessity permits relaxation of normal requirements.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin, as later Hanafi scholars have affirmed this concession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ضرَّه غسل شقوق رجليه جاز امرار الماء على هـ م عـجـال فانتهينا اليهم و اعقابهم تلوح لم يمسها الماء فقال رسول الله عله ويل للاعقاب من النار أسبغوا الوضوء . (مسلم شریف، باب وجوب غسل الرجلین بکمالها ص ۱۲۰، نمبر ۵۷۰/۲۴۱) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کھال کا تھوڑا ساحصہ بھی سوکھا نہیں رہنا چاہئے ورنہ وضو نہیں ہوگا۔ تشریح : پاؤں یا ہاتھ پھٹ گیا ہے جسکی وجہ سے اس پر اہم رکھا یوکوئی دوائی رکھی تو اس پر صرف پانی گزار دینے سے وضو ہو جائے گا۔ چاہے اندر تک پانی پہنچے یانہ پہنچے۔ وجه : (۱) یہاں پھٹن کی مجبوری ہے اس لئے جسم کو رگڑ کر اندر تک پانی پہنچانا ضروری نہیں ہے، جس طرح کچیچی بندھی ہوئی ہو تو اس پر پانی سے مسح کر لینا کافی ہے، زخم کو دھونا ضروری نہیں اسی طرح یہاں بھی ہوگا۔ (۲) اس حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن جابر قال : خرجنا في سفر .... انـمـا يكفيه ان يتيمم و يعصر او یعصب شک موسیى على جرحه خرقه ثم يمسح
And if washing the cracks of his feet harms him, it is permissible to pass water over... [garbled] then we came to them and their heels were dry, water had not touched them. The Messenger of Allah said: Woe to the heels from the Fire. Perfect the wudu. (Muslim, no. 570/241). This hadith indicates that even a small part of skin must not remain dry, otherwise wudu is invalid. Explanation: The foot or hand is cracked, and because of that there is a bandage or medicine on it; then just passing water over it will suffice for wudu, whether the water reaches inside or not. Evidence: (1) Here there is the constraint of cracking, so it is not necessary to rub the body to make water reach inside, just like when a wound is bandaged, wiping over it with water is sufficient, washing the wound is not necessary; similarly here. (2) This is proven by the hadith: From Jabir, he said: We went on a journey... it suffices him to do tayammum and... [garbled] Musa on his wound a cloth and then wipe.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is a ruling_with_proof passage. The author states that if washing cracked feet causes harm, then merely passing water over the bandage or medicine is permissible. The explanation analogizes to the case of a bandaged wound where wiping over the bandage suffices. The hadith evidence is partially garbled but mentions a similar concession. The passage emphasizes that in cases of harm, the requirement to reach the skin is relaxed.

Key Terms
شقوق (shuquq): Cracks in the skin, especially on feet or hands, which may be painful to wash.
الجبائر (al-jabā'ir): Bandages or splints; here used for applying medicine on cracks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is permissible to pass water over the bandage/medicine.

D) The crack must be left dry.

Key Takeaway
When washing a cracked or wounded area causes harm, passing water over the covering (bandage or medicine) suffices for the validity of wudu.

Removing Waterproof Barriers during Wudu
Ruling on dough-like substance requiring washing underneath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a substance is considered a barrier (yamna‘ al-mā’) and state the obligation to remove it and wash the skin underneath.

Core Idea
Any substance that blocks water from the skin must be removed before wudu.

In this lesson we examine a concise ruling from the matn: if a substance like dough (a thick, waterproof paste) sticks to a limb during wudu, it must be removed and the skin underneath washed. This principle applies to any barrier that prevents water from reaching the required area. We will break down the conditions and implications of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The passage makes a conditional statement: if a substance prevents water (like dough), then it is obligatory (wajib) to wash what is under it. The act here is the removal of the barrier and subsequent washing.
The ruling is wajib, not fard, because it is a condition for the validity of wudu, not a separate act of ibadah.
2
Define the Condition
The condition is that the substance 'prevents water' (yamna‘ al-mā’). This means it creates a layer impermeable to water, such that water cannot reach the skin underneath.
Dough (al-‘ajīn) is the example; other solid or semi-solid substances like glue, wax, or dried paint also qualify.
3
Determine the Consequence of Omission
If such a barrier is present and not removed, the water does not reach the skin, so that part of the limb is considered unwashed, invalidating the wudu.
Therefore, the barrier must be removed before or during washing.
4
Apply to Similar Cases
The ruling extends by qiyas to any substance that creates a waterproof barrier, even if transparent or colorless, like clear nail polish or silicone-based barriers.
The key is prevention of water, not visibility or impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنع الماء كعجين وجب غسل ما تحته
If it prevents water, like dough, it is obligatory to wash what is under it.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn that if a substance such as dough (a thick paste) sits on the skin and prevents water from reaching it during wudu, then it is obligatory to remove that substance and wash the skin underneath. The ruling is based on the obligation that water must reach all parts of the wudu limbs. Any external barrier that blocks water must be removed. The example of dough indicates any similar thick substance that forms a waterproof layer.

Key Terms
يمنع (yamna'): Prevents, blocks; specifically refers to any substance that creates a barrier preventing water from reaching the skin.
كعجين (ka-'ajin): Like dough; a thick, cohesive substance that forms a waterproof layer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If dough is stuck on the forearm during wudu, what must one do?
A) Leave it and wipe over it.
B) Remove it and wash the skin underneath.
C) Wet it without removing.
D) It is not necessary to wash that spot.

Key Takeaway
Any waterproof barrier on the skin must be removed before wudu; otherwise, the wudu is invalid.

Dirt and Flea Droppings: When Small Impurities Do Not Block Water
Ruling statement: dirt and flea droppings do not block water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that light dirt and flea droppings do not require removal for wudu, and explain the reasoning using qiyas.

Core Idea
Thin dirt and flecks do not block water; wudu remains valid.

This short but powerful matn establishes a key principle in taharah: not every impurity prevents water from reaching the skin. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling, its evidence, and its practical implications. Understanding when a substance is considered a barrier (mani') versus negligible is essential for correct wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the text deals with wudu and states that certain substances do not prevent water from reaching the skin. The ruling is that wudu remains valid despite their presence.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is qiyas (analogy): these substances are akin to perspiration, which also does not block water. They are thin and porous.
Dalil type: Qiyas; thubut: zanni; dalala: zanni
3
Note the implicit condition
The ruling only applies when the dirt or droppings are thin and do not form a waterproof layer. If they were thick, they would be a barrier and must be removed.
The text uses 'al-daran' meaning light dirt, not thick mud.
4
Consider the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that any foreign matter on the skin should be removed as a precaution, but the Hanafi school relies on custom ('urf) to treat negligible amounts as excusable.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (mukhtalaf fih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمنع الدرن وخرء البراغيث ونحوها
And dirt and flea droppings and the like do not prevent.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is a ruling that dirt (light filth) and droppings of fleas and similar small insects do not prevent water from reaching the skin. The reason is that these are typically very thin or porous, not forming a waterproof barrier. Thus, wudu is valid even if they are present on the skin. The commentary explains that they are minimal and do not hinder water.

Key Terms
الدرن (al-daran): Dirt, grime; light filth on the skin that does not form a thick layer.
خرء البراغيث (khar' al-baraghith): Flea droppings; small, dry excrement that does not block water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Wudu is valid as long as the dirt does not block water.
C) Wudu is valid only if the dirt is unavoidable.
D) Wudu is valid but disliked.

Key Takeaway
Thin, non-waterproof dirt and similar substances do not invalidate wudu in the Hanafi school, as they do not block water from reaching the skin.

Core Idea
A tight ring must be moved during wudu to ensure water reaches the skin.

In the Hanafi school, wudu requires that water reach every part of the limbs. Any obstruction that prevents this, such as a tight ring, must be addressed. This lesson examines the ruling and its evidence from the practice of the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is moving a tight ring during wudu. The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
The text explicitly states 'wajib' (يجب) in Arabic.
2
Locate the dalil

The hadith is recorded by Ibn Majah and Tirmidhi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah

Hanafi usul holds that the Prophet's continuous actions with explanatory context indicate obligation.
4
Note the scope
This ruling applies only when the ring is tight (al-dayyiq). If the ring is loose, moving it is not obligatory because water can reach underneath.
The condition of tightness is derived from the purpose of the ruling.

Principle: Any Dry Spot Invalidates Wudu
Principle that any dry spot invalidates wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on complete washing of wudu limbs and apply the principle that any dry area invalidates wudu.

Core Idea
Leaving even a single dry spot on wudu limbs invalidates the entire wudu.

This lesson establishes a fundamental principle of wudu: every part of the limbs must be completely washed. The proof comes from a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ warned about dry heels. The Hanafi school holds this obligation as wajib, meaning omission invalidates wudu. Let's examine the proof and the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (wudu) and what level of obligation is assigned (wajib).
Wajib means obligatory; omission invalidates the act.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith from Sahih Muslim about the Prophet's warning regarding dry heels.
This hadith is the primary textual evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling. The Prophet's command 'asbaghu al-wudu' (perform wudu completely) and his warning indicate that any unwashed part is sinful and invalidates wudu.
The explicit warning about the Fire shows the severity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Check if there is any disagreement. This principle is agreed upon within the Hanafi school and is also accepted by the majority of scholars.
Muttafaq alayh (agreed upon) across madhahib.
5
Extract the principle
From the hadith and commentary, derive the ruling: any dry spot on the wudu limbs invalidates wudu.
The principle is absolute; no exception for small areas.

Washing Cracked Feet When Harmful
Ruling on passing water over cracked feet if washing harms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing cracked feet during wudu and identify the analogous proof from the hadith on bandaged wounds.

Core Idea
When washing a wound or crack causes harm, passing water suffices — a mercy from the Lawgiver.

Wudu requires washing the feet, but what if one has deep cracks or fissures that make scrubbing painful? Islamic law does not demand hardship. This lesson explores the Hanafi concession that allows simply passing water over such cracks, drawing on prophetic hadith and analogy to wound care.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing cracked feet during wudu. The ruling is a rukhsah (concession): if washing causes harm, it is permissible to merely pass water over the cracks (imrar al-ma').
The base obligation (washing the feet) remains, but the method is relaxed.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith warning about heels not being washed ("Woe to the heels from the Fire"), emphasizing the necessity of washing all parts. Additionally, the ruling is extended by qiyas to the hadith of wiping over a bandaged wound (Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah).
The wound hadith establishes that when washing a wound harms, wiping over the covering suffices.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the presence of harm or difficulty. When washing intensifies pain or risks injury, the Lawgiver permits a lighter action. In the wound hadith, the Prophet allowed wiping over the bandage; here, passing water over cracks is analogous.
The cracks are treated like an open wound.
4
Note the scope and conditions
The concession applies only when actual harm (darr) can be reasonably expected. If no harm, the feet must be washed normally. The cracks must be deep enough to qualify as wounds.
Superficial dryness does not justify this ruling.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
This is the standard Hanafi position recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah. Later authorities (e.g., al-Haskafi, Ibn Abidin) uphold it. There is no significant intra-Hanafi disagreement.
The position is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ضره غسل شقوق رجليه جاز امرار الماء على هـم عـجـال فانتهينا اليهم و اعقابهم تلوح لم يمسها الماء فقال رسول الله عله ويل للاعقاب من النار أسبغوا الوضوء
And if washing the cracks of his feet harms him, it is permissible to pass water over ... Then we reached them and their heels were gleaming, water had not touched them, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Woe to the heels from the Fire, perform wudu properly.'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This ruling addresses a person with cracked feet (deep fissures). If washing the inside of the cracks causes pain or harm, it suffices to merely pass water over them without rubbing. The proof is the hadith about neglect of heels, emphasizing the need to wash all parts. Additionally, the commentary compares it to a bandaged wound where wiping over the bandage suffices, citing hadith from Jabir and Ali. Thus, the general principle is that when washing a wound harms, passing water over it is acceptable.

Key Terms
شقوق (shuqooq): Cracks or fissures in the skin, especially on feet, that make washing painful.
امرار الماء (imrar al-ma'): Passing water over the area without rubbing or forcing it into cracks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Force water into the cracks even if it hurts.
D) Skip washing the feet entirely.

Key Takeaway
When washing cracked feet causes harm, the Hanafi school permits merely passing water over them, drawing on the analogy of wiping over a bandaged wound.

Repeating Wudu After Shaving Hair or Cutting Nails
Ruling that shaving/cutting does not require repeating wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that shaving hair or cutting nails does not require repeating wudu and identify the evidence from the Companion Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
Wudu is not invalidated by shaving hair or trimming nails; the previous washing suffices.

It is a common question whether removing hair or nails after wudu breaks the purification. This lesson clarifies that such actions do not require a fresh wudu, based on the practice of the Companion Ibn Umar. Understanding this saves needless repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that after performing wudu, if one shaves hair (head or body) or cuts nails/moustache, the wudu remains valid. There is no need to re-wipe or rewash those areas.
2
Locate the evidence (dalil)
The proof is the action of Ibn Umar: when asked after trimming his nails if he would perform wudu, he replied emphatically no, indicating that cutting nails does not invalidate wudu.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi (statement/action of a Companion), which is a zanni (probative) source.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The reason is that the previous washing already covered those parts. Cutting or shaving does not introduce new impurity; it simply exposes skin that was already washed. Thus, no repetition is required.
4
Identify the scope of the ruling
This ruling applies to shaving any hair (head, beard, armpits, etc.) and cutting nails or trimming the moustache. It does not apply to acts that actually break wudu, like passing wind.
Common misunderstanding: some think the exposed part needs rewashing; this ruling negates that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعاد المسح ولا الغسل على موضع الشعر بعد حلقه ولا الغسل بقص ظفره وشاربه
And wiping nor washing is repeated on the place of hair after shaving it, nor washing by cutting his nail and moustache.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that if a person has already performed wudu and then shaves their head (or any body hair), or cuts their nails or moustache, they do not need to redo the wudu or wash those areas again. Wudu is not invalidated by such actions. The proof is the athar from Ibn Umar: when asked after trimming nails if he would redo wudu, he said no, and from Hasan al-Basri that nothing is required. This is because the previous washing already sufficed for those parts.

Key Terms
قص (qass): Cutting or trimming; refers to nails and moustache.
حلقه (halq): Shaving; specifically hair from the head or body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Rewash his hands.
B) Perform a new wudu.
C) Nothing; his wudu is still valid.
D) Wipe over the cut area.

Key Takeaway
Shaving hair or cutting nails after wudu does not break the wudu; the previous washing is sufficient.

Shaving Hair and Cutting Nails Does Not Break Wudu
Ruling with proof on not repeating wudu after cutting hair/nails

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that cutting nails and shaving hair do not nullify wudu and identify the proof from the actions of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
Cutting nails or hair does not break wudu — the ruling is based on clear practice of the Salaf.

Many people wonder whether personal grooming acts like shaving or nail-cutting affect the validity of wudu. This passage from a classical Hanafi text provides a clear ruling: they do not. The text supports this with actions and sayings of early Muslims, emphasizing that wudu remains intact after such acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that after shaving hair, cutting nails, or trimming the mustache, one does not need to repeat wiping or washing. The ruling is that wudu is not invalidated by these acts.
The ruling is a negative statement: no renewal required.
2
Locate the primary proof
The first proof is the action of Ibn Umar (a Companion) who cut his nails and did not perform wudu again. He questioned why he would need to, indicating that this was common knowledge.
The proof is from a qawl al-sahabi (saying of a Companion).
3
Examine the secondary proof
The second proof is the statement of al-Hasan al-Basri (a Tabi'i) that there is no obligation to repeat wudu after trimming nails or hair.
Although a Tabi'i saying is weaker, it supports the Companion practice.
4
Note the Hanafi position and its weight
The Hanafi school relies on these proofs and considers the ruling from the Zahir al-Riwayah. It is considered a clear position, though some early scholars had differing views.
The authority weight is 'zahir_riwayah'.
5
Address potential misunderstanding
Some people mistakenly think such acts require a fresh wudu. The passage clarifies that only the standard nullifiers (e.g., breaking wind, using the toilet) break wudu.
The act of cutting does not cause anything to exit or enter the body.

Translation: (83) Do not repeat wiping or washing on the place of hair after shaving it. And there is no need to repeat washing after cutting nail and mustache. Explanation: If one already had wudu and shaved hair or cut nails or trimmed mustache, wudu is not broken, so there is no need to repeat wudu. This passage is a ruling with proof establishing that shaving hair, cutting nails, or trimming the mustache does not nullify wudu. The Eighteen Sunnah Acts of Wudu
Definition and list of 18 sunnah acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 18 sunnah acts of wudu and distinguish between sunnah and mustahabb in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Eighteen sunnah acts complete wudu; omission does not invalidate.

This lesson presents the 18 sunnah acts of wudu as enumerated in the Hanafi school. Understanding which acts are sunnah (emphasized recommended) versus mustahabb (habitual) is key to proper practice. The passage defines these terms and lists the acts to remember.

Guided Path
1
Define sunnah and mustahabb
Understand from the passage that sunnah is an act the Prophet consistently performed as worship, occasionally leaving it. Mustahabb is a habitual act, like starting with the right. This distinction affects the ruling: sunnah brings reward but omission is not sinful.
Hanafi fiqh categorizes sunnah muakkadah as a separate level between wajib and mustahabb.
2
Recite the 18 sunnah acts
Memorize the list as given: washing hands to wrists, Bismillah, miswak (or finger), rinsing mouth three times, sniffing water three times, exaggerating if not fasting, khilal of beard and fingers, washing each limb three times, wiping head once, wiping ears, washing successively, intention, and following order.
Some acts like khilal and succession are specific sunnah; others like intention are also sunnah in Hanafi wudu.
3
Distinguish sunnah from wajib and fard
In wudu, fard (obligatory) acts must be performed; wajib (necessary) acts require compensation if missed; sunnah acts are recommended but not required. Omitting a sunnah does not break wudu or require sajdah al-sahw.
Hanafis hold that the four fardh acts of wudu are washing face, hands, wiping head, and washing feet.
4
Apply the list in practice
When performing wudu, intend to follow the sunnah. Perform each act in order, observing the recommended number of times. For example, say Bismillah, then wash hands thrice, then rinse mouth thrice, etc.
The list is a memorization anchor; actual performance may vary slightly but the core acts remain.

Chapter on the sunnah acts of wudu. Chapter: Eighteen things are sunnah in wudu. Important note: Sunnah acts of wudu: the way of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is called sunnah. In Shariah, an act that the Prophet consistently performed as an act of worship, occasionally leaving it, is called sunnah. If he consistently did an act out of habit, not worship, it is mustahabb, like starting with the right side. The entire chapter at a glance: 18 sunnah acts of wudu at a glance: ... washing both hands up to the wrists; reciting Bismillah at the beginning; using miswak, or if no miswak, using the finger; rinsing (the mouth) three times; taking water into the nose with three handfuls; if not fasting, exaggerating in rinsing and sniffing; passing fingers through a thick beard; passing fingers between all fingers; washing each limb three times; wiping the entire head once; wiping both ears with the water of the head; washing the body (parts) ... washing successively; intention; performing in order according to the text of the Book of Allah.

Commentary
This passage presents the list of 18 sunnah acts in wudu, which are recommended but not obligatory. The text begins by defining the term 'sunnah' as the Prophet's consistent practice as an act of worship, occasionally omitted. It distinguishes between sunnah (worship-related) and mustahabb (habitual). The 18 acts include washing hands to wrists, saying Bismillah, using miswak, rinsing the mouth three times, sniffing water three times, exaggerating if not fasting, khilal of beard and fingers, washing limbs three times, wiping head once, wiping ears, washing successively, intention, and following order. Students should memorize these as they complete the wudu and bring reward, while omission does not invalidate wudu.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): An act the Prophet consistently performed as worship, occasionally leaving it.
مستحب (mustahabb): An act the Prophet consistently did out of habit, not as worship, such as starting with the right.

Sunnah of Washing Hands to Wrists
Proving the sunnah of washing hands to wrists before wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of washing hands to wrists in wudu and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Washing hands to wrists is sunnah, not fard — it ensures water purity but does not invalidate wudu if missed.

In this lesson, we examine the first sunnah of wudu: washing both hands up to the wrists. The Hanafi school classifies this act as sunnah muakkadah, recommended but not obligatory. The text provides both the rationale (removing impurity) and the evidence from the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). Understanding the distinction between sunnah and fard is crucial for correct wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? Washing both hands to the wrists before beginning wudu. The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (confirmed sunnah).
Sunnah muakkadah means the Prophet consistently performed it; omission is not sinful but loses reward.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadith are cited: the detailed wudu of Uthman (Bukhari, Abu Dawud) and the ghusl of Aisha (Muslim). Both show the Prophet began by washing his hands.
The hadith are ahad (single transmitted) but authentic.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase 'فبدأ فغسل كفيه' (he began by washing his hands) indicates the Prophet's practice. Hanafi usul holds that such consistent action establishes sunniyyah, not wujub.
Because the action is not commanded explicitly, it remains sunnah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools might interpret the same hadith as indicating obligation. Hanafi distinguishes based on the strength of proof (zanni) and absence of command.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is that it is sunnah.
5
Practical implication
If one omits washing hands to wrists, the wudu is still valid. However, the sunnah reward is missed, and if hands are impure, the water used may become impure.
The wisdom is to purify the hands before using the wudu water.

(84) Washing both hands to the wrists. (85) Saying bismillah at the beginning. (86) Using siwak at the beginning, even with the finger. Translation: (84) Among the eighteen things that are sunnah in wudu: [1] Washing both hands up to the wrists. Explanation: Washing hands up to the wrists before starting wudu is sunnah so that if there is impurity on the hands, it is washed away and the water does not become impure. Proof: (1) That Humran, the freed slave of Uthman, informed him that he saw Uthman bin Affan call for a vessel, then he poured water on his hands three times and washed them, then he put his right hand into the vessel, rinsed his mouth and nose, then washed his face three times and his hands to the elbows three times, then wiped his head, then washed his feet three times to the ankles. Then he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever performs wudu like my wudu, then prays two rak'ahs without talking to himself, his previous sins will be forgiven.' (Bukhari, Bab al-Wudu Thalathan Thalathan, p.32 no.159; Abu Dawud, Bab Sifat Wudu al-Nabi, p.26 no.108) In this hadith, he first washed his hands three times to the wrists. (2) Also in this hadith from Aisha that the Prophet performed ghusl from janabah and began by washing his hands three times. (Muslim, Bab Sifat Ghusl al-Janabah, p.143 no.720/316) In this hadith, he first washed his hands three times to the wrists.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof establishing the first sunnah of wudu: washing both hands up to the wrists before beginning wudu. The author explains that this act is sunnah, not fard, and the wisdom is to remove any impurity from the hands so that the water used for wudu remains pure. Then he provides two hadith proofs. The first is a detailed description of the Prophet's wudu as reported by Uthman, where he began by washing his hands three times to the wrists. The second is from Aisha, concerning ghusl, where the Prophet also started by washing his hands. Both hadiths are authentic (Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud). The Hanafi school uses these to prove that washing the hands to the wrists is a recommended sunnah before starting wudu, not an obligatory part. The practice ensures that the water does not become contaminated by any impurity on the hands.

Key Terms
رسغ (rusgh): The wrist joint, specifically the point where the hand meets the forearm.

D) To make the water impure.

Key Takeaway
Washing hands to wrists before wudu is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah muakkadah) that ensures water purity but does not invalidate wudu if omitted.

Sunnah of Reciting Bismillah in Wudu
Proving the sunnah of reciting bismillah in wudu through hadith and athar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reciting Bismillah in wudu and explain how the hadith and athar support the sunnah position.

Core Idea
Reciting bismillah is sunnah, not wajib — the hadith 'no wudu' means no perfect reward, not invalidation.

This lesson explores the second sunnah of wudu: reciting Bismillah. The author presents two key proofs — a hadith and an athar from al-Hasan — and shows how the Hanafi school derives the ruling of sunnah from them. You will learn to distinguish between apparent obligation and actual recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling sought
The act is reciting bismillah at the start of wudu. Determine its hukum from the text.
2
Examine the primary proof — the hadith
The hadith states 'لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله عليه'. At face value, it negates wudu itself.
3
Interpret the hadith in light of other evidence
The athar from al-Hasan says wudu is sufficient even without bismillah. This forces a reinterpretation of the hadith.
Hanafi principle: combine proofs when possible; the athar overrides the literal meaning of the hadith here.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle of combining proofs
The negation in the hadith is interpreted as 'no complete wudu' (i.e., no perfect reward), not invalidity.
5
State the final ruling
Thus, bismillah is sunnah muakkadah. Omitting it does not invalidate wudu but loses reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۸۵) شروع میں بسم اللہ پڑھے۔
وجه : (ا) ابي سفيان بن حويطب عن جدتة عن ابيها قال سمعت رسول الله عله يقول لا وضوء لمن لم يذكر اسم الله علیه ترندی شریف، باب فی التسمیة عند الوضوء ، ص ۱۳، نمبر ۲۵ / ابوداؤ د شریف، باب فی التسمية على الوضوء ص ۸ نمبر (۱۰) حدیث میں یہ ذکر ہے کہ بغیر بسم اللہ کے وضوء ہی نہیں ہوگا۔ لیکن اس سے مراد ہے کہ وضوء تو ہو جائے گا لیکن ثواب نہیں ملے گا۔ (۲) عن الحسن قال : يسمى اذا توضأ ، فان لم يفعل اجزاه - (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ، باب فی التسمية في الوضوء، ج اول ،ص ۱۲، نمبر ۱۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ بغیر بسم اللہ کے بھی وضو ہو جائے گا ، اس لئے بسم اللہ سنت ہے۔
Translation: (85) Recite bismillah at the beginning. Proof: (1) From Abu Sufyan bin Huwaytib from his grandmother from her father: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'There is no wudu for the one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it.' (Tirmidhi, Bab al-Tasmiyah ind al-Wudu, p.13 no.25; Abu Dawud, Bab al-Tasmiyah ala al-Wudu, p.8 no.10) In this hadith, it is mentioned that without bismillah there will be no wudu. But the intended meaning is that wudu will be valid but no reward will be given. (2) From al-Hasan: He said: 'He should say bismillah when he performs wudu, and if he does not do it, it is sufficient for him.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, Bab al-Tasmiyah fi al-Wudu, vol.1, p.12 no.18) This effect shows that wudu is valid without bismillah, therefore bismillah is sunnah.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof establishing the second sunnah of wudu: reciting bismillah at the beginning. The author presents two proofs. The first is a hadith from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud: 'There is no wudu for the one who does not mention Allah's name.' At first glance, this seems to indicate obligation, but the Hanafi scholars interpret it as meaning 'no complete wudu' i.e., no perfect reward, not that the wudu is invalid. The second proof is an athar (statement) from al-Hasan al-Basri: if one forgets, the wudu is sufficient (jaza). The Hanafi position is that bismillah is sunnah, not wajib or fard. This is based on the principle that any command that is not definitive in meaning is interpreted as recommendation, especially when there is evidence from the Salaf that the act is not invalidated by its omission. The athar from al-Hasan directly supports this. Therefore, reciting bismillah is a recommended act that brings reward, but its omission does not affect the validity of wudu.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiyah): Reciting bismillah (بسم الله) at the beginning of an act.
اثر (athar): A saying or action of a Companion or Successor, used as secondary evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do Hanafis interpret the hadith 'There is no wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name'?
A) The wudu is invalid without bismillah.
B) The wudu is valid but has no reward.
C) The wudu is valid but must be repeated.
D) The wudu is incomplete and requires repetition.

Key Takeaway
Reciting bismillah in wudu is a highly recommended sunnah, not an obligation, and its omission does not affect the validity of wudu.

Siwak as Sunnah of Wudu
Proving the sunnah of using siwak at the beginning of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using siwak during wudu and explain when it is performed and what substitutes if siwak is unavailable.

Core Idea
Siwak is a sunnah of wudu, performed at its start; if unavailable, one uses a finger.

This lesson examines the third sunnah of wudu: using a siwak (tooth-stick). The Hanafi school uniquely ties siwak to wudu rather than prayer, emphasizing oral purification as part of wudu's sunan. You will learn the ruling, its timing, and the substitute when a siwak is not available.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is using a siwak at the beginning of wudu. The hukm level in Hanafi fiqh is sunnah muakkadah — a confirmed sunnah.
Sunnah muakkadah is a practice the Prophet consistently performed; omission is disliked but not sinful.
2
Understand the substitute provision
Note that if a siwak is not available, one may use a finger to perform the cleaning motion. This preserves the sunnah spirit when the ideal tool is absent.
The substitute (finger) is considered a minimal fulfillment; using a toothbrush or similar would also suffice.
3
Locate the Hanafi timing
The text specifies that Hanafis perform siwak before wudu, not before prayer. This distinguishes them from other schools who may recommend siwak immediately before prayer.
The illa (reason) is that wudu involves purification of the entire body including the mouth, so siwak is linked to wudu.
4
Contrast with other schools
Some scholars recommend siwak at the time of prayer. The Hanafi position restricts it to wudu, because the Prophet consistently used siwak when making wudu.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (difference) within the Ummah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۸۶) شروع میں مسواک کرنا ، اور مسواک نہ ہوتے وقت انگلی سے مسواک کرنا۔
تشریح : مسواک میسر ہو تو مسواک کرے اور وہ نہ ہو تو کم از کم انگلی سے مسواک کا عمل کرے، حنفیہ کے یہاں یہ ہے کہ وضو سے پہلے کرے نماز سے پہلے نہیں۔
Translation: (86) Using siwak at the beginning, and if no siwak is available, then with the finger. Explanation: If siwak is available, one should use it, and if not, then at least perform the action of siwak with the finger. In the Hanafi school, this is done before wudu, not before prayer.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof establishing the third sunnah of wudu: using a siwak (tooth-stick) at the beginning of wudu. The author rules that if a siwak is available, it should be used; if not, one can use a finger to mimic the cleaning action. A distinctive Hanafi position is that the siwak in the context of wudu is performed before wudu, not before prayer. This is because the siwak is a sunnah of wudu, purifying the mouth, and the wudu itself precedes prayer. Some other schools may recommend siwak specifically before prayer, but Hanafis link it to wudu. The text does not provide an explicit proof here, but it refers to the general practice of the Prophet. The ruling is clear: siwak is a sunnah act, not obligatory, and its substitute (finger) suffices when a real siwak is unavailable.

Key Terms
سواك (siwak): A tooth-cleaning stick from the arak tree, used for oral hygiene and as a sunnah act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when should one use the siwak in relation to wudu?
A) After wudu, before prayer.
B) At the beginning of wudu.
C) Only during prayer.
D) It is not related to wudu.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, using the siwak at the start of wudu is a sunnah muakkadah; if unavailable, one uses a finger as substitute.

Sunnahs of Wudu: Washing Hands, Bismillah, Miswak, Mouth and Nose
Enumerates six sunnahs of wudu with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six sunnahs of wudu according to the Hanafi school, cite the hadith proofs for each, and explain why they are classified as sunnah rather than wajib.

Core Idea
Six acts are sunnah in wudu — missing them does not invalidate but loses reward.

This lesson presents the core sunnahs of wudu as taught in the Hanafi tradition. Understanding these sunnahs helps you perform wudu with the full prophetic model, but also clarifies that the obligatory elements of wudu are separate. The author of Thamaratun Najah lists six specific sunnahs with detailed hadith evidence, showing how the Hanafi school derives recommended acts from the Prophet's practice and statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah act
Read each numbered sunnah in the passage. Name the act clearly (e.g., 'washing hands up to wrists').
2
Locate the primary dalil
For each sunnah, find the hadith or athar the author cites as evidence. Note the textual reference when given (e.g., Bukhari, Muslim).
Some sunnahs have multiple proofs.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
Examine how the dalil indicates the act is sunnah, not wajib. For example, the hadith 'no wudu without Bismillah' is interpreted to mean valid but without reward.
The author often provides the reasoning directly.
4
Note conditions and exceptions
Identify any special rulings, such as mubalaghah being sunnah only for non-fasting persons.
These conditions are part of the full legal picture.
5
Distinguish cross-madhhab views
Note where the Hanafi position differs from other schools, e.g., the timing of miswak. The author addresses these differences.
This step helps solidify the Hanafi reliance on specific evidence.
